Justitia carries symbolic items such as: a sword, <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale#Balance" title="Weighing scale">scales</a> and a blindfold<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="2350" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg/220px-Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg/330px-Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg/440px-Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justice_Alberi_Palazzo_Altemps.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Justice, one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_cardinal_virtues" title="Four cardinal virtues">four cardinal virtues</a>, by Vitruvio Alberi, 1589–1590. Fresco, corner of the vault, studiolo of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_of_Mercy" title="Virgin of Mercy">Madonna of Mercy</a>, Palazzo Altemps, Rome</div></div></div>
<p><b>Justice</b>, in its broadest sense, is the principle  that people receive that which they deserve, with the interpretation of what then constitutes "deserving" being impacted upon by numerous fields, with many differing viewpoints and perspectives, including the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral</a> correctness based on <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equity_(law)" title="Equity (law)">equity</a> and fairness. The state will sometimes endeavour to increase justice by operating <a href="/wiki/Court" title="Court">courts</a> and enforcing their rulings.
</p><p>Early theories of justice were set out by the Ancient Greek philosophers <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in his work <a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">The Republic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a>. Advocates of divine command theory have said that justice issues from God. In the 1600s, philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> said that justice derives from <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>. <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">Social contract</a> theory said that justice is derived from the mutual agreement of everyone. In the 1800s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utilitarian" title="Utilitarian">utilitarian</a> philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> said that justice is based on the best outcomes for the greatest number of people. Theories of <a href="/wiki/Distributive_justice" title="Distributive justice">distributive justice</a> study what is to be distributed, between whom they are to be distributed, and what is the <i>proper</i> distribution. <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">Egalitarians</a> have said that justice can only exist within the coordinates of equality. <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">John Rawls</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory to say that justice, and especially distributive justice, is a form of fairness. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nozick" title="Robert Nozick">Robert Nozick</a> and others said that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Property_rights" title="Property rights">property rights</a>, also within the realm of distributive justice and natural law, maximizes the overall wealth of an economic system. Theories of <a href="/wiki/Retributive_justice" title="Retributive justice">retributive justice</a> say that wrongdoing should be <a href="/wiki/Punishment" title="Punishment">punished</a> to ensure justice. The closely related <a href="/wiki/Restorative_justice" title="Restorative justice">restorative justice</a> (also sometimes called "reparative justice") is an approach to justice that focuses on the needs of victims and offenders.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmony">Harmony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Harmony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Republic_(Plato)" title="The Republic (Plato)">The Republic (Plato)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luca_Giordano_013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3560" data-file-width="2536" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Luca_Giordano_013.jpg/220px-Luca_Giordano_013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Luca_Giordano_013.jpg/330px-Luca_Giordano_013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Luca_Giordano_013.jpg/440px-Luca_Giordano_013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luca_Giordano_013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Justice</i> by <a href="/wiki/Luca_Giordano" title="Luca Giordano">Luca Giordano</a></div></div></div>
<p>Justice is a proper, harmonious relationship between the warring parts of the person or city. Hence, Plato's definition of justice is that justice is the having and doing of what is one's own. A just man is a man in just the right place, doing his best and giving the precise equivalent of what he has received. This applies both at the individual level and at the universal level. A person's soul has three parts – reason, spirit and desire. Similarly, a city has three parts – Socrates uses the parable of the chariot to illustrate his point: a chariot works as a whole because the two horses' power is directed by the charioteer. Lovers of wisdom – philosophers, in one sense of the term – <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_king" title="Philosopher king">should rule</a> because only they understand what is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goodness_and_evil" title="Goodness and evil">good</a>. If one is ill, one goes to a medic rather than a farmer, because the medic is expert in the subject of health. Similarly, one should trust one's city to an expert in the subject of the good, not to a mere <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">politician</a> who tries to gain power by giving people what they want, rather than what's good for them. Socrates uses the parable of the ship to illustrate this point: the unjust city is like a ship in open ocean, crewed by a powerful but drunken captain (the common people), a group of untrustworthy advisors who try to manipulate the captain into giving them power over the ship's course (the politicians), and a <a href="/wiki/Navigator" title="Navigator">navigator</a> (the philosopher) who is the only one who knows how to get the ship to port. For Socrates, the only way the ship will reach its destination – the good – is if the navigator takes charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_command">Divine command</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Divine command">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Divine_command_theory" title="Divine command theory">Divine command theory</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_command" title="Divine command">Divine command</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1956" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg/220px-Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg/330px-Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg/440px-Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Domenico_Beccafumi_012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Allegorical fresco cycle (cardinal virtues) by <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> painter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domenico_di_Pace_Beccafumi" title="Domenico di Pace Beccafumi">Domenico di Pace Beccafumi</a> from the Palazzo Pubblico in Siena, scene: ’'Justitia'’</div></div></div>
<p>Advocates of divine command theory say that justice, and indeed the whole of morality, is the authoritative command of God. Murder is wrong and must be punished, for instance, because God says it so. Some versions of the theory assert that God must be obeyed because of the nature of his relationship with humanity, others assert that God must be obeyed because he is goodness itself, and thus doing what he says would be best for everyone.
</p><p>A meditation on the Divine command theory by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> can be found in his dialogue, <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro" title="Euthyphro">Euthyphro</a>. Called the <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro_dilemma" title="Euthyphro dilemma">Euthyphro dilemma</a>, it goes as follows: "Is what is morally good commanded by God because it is morally good, or is it morally good because it is commanded by God?" The implication is that if the latter is true, then justice is beyond mortal understanding; if the former is true, then morality exists independently from God, and is therefore subject to the judgment of mortals. A <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_morality" title="Argument from morality">response</a>, popularized in two contexts by <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> and <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>, is that it is deductively valid to say that the existence of an objective morality implies the existence of God and vice versa.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_law">Natural law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Natural law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VD-04-3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/VD-04-3.jpg/220px-VD-04-3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/VD-04-3.jpg/330px-VD-04-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/VD-04-3.jpg/440px-VD-04-3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VD-04-3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Lex, justitia, pax</i> (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "Law, justice, peace") on the pediment of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Supreme_Court_of_Switzerland" title="Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland">Supreme Court of Switzerland</a></div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p>For advocates of the theory that justice is part of natural law (e.g., <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>), justice involves the nature of man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Despotism_and_skepticism">Despotism and skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Despotism and skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <i>Republic</i> by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, the character <a href="/wiki/Thrasymachus" title="Thrasymachus">Thrasymachus</a> argues that justice is the interest of the strong – merely a name for what the powerful or cunning ruler has imposed on the people.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Republic_(Plato)" title="The Republic (Plato)">The Republic (Plato)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutual_agreement">Mutual agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mutual agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">Social contract</a></div>
<p>Advocates of the social contract say that justice is derived from the mutual agreement of everyone; or, in many versions, from what they would agree to under <i>hypothetical</i> conditions including equality and absence of bias. This account is considered further below, under '<a href="/wiki/Justice_as_Fairness" title="Justice as Fairness">Justice as Fairness</a>'. The absence of bias refers to an equal ground for all people involved in a disagreement (or trial in some cases).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subordinate_value">Subordinate value</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Subordinate value">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to utilitarian thinkers including <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, justice is not as fundamental as we often think. Rather, it is derived from the more basic standard of rightness, <a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">consequentialism</a>: what is right is what has the best consequences (usually measured by the total or average <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">welfare</a> caused). So, the proper principles of justice are those that tend to have the best consequences. These rules may turn out to be familiar ones such as keeping <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contracts</a>; but equally, they may not, depending on the facts about real consequences. Either way, what is important is those consequences, and justice is important, if at all, only as derived from that fundamental standard. Mill tries to explain our mistaken belief that justice is overwhelmingly important by arguing that it derives from two natural human tendencies: our desire to retaliate against those who hurt us, or the feeling of self-defense and our ability to put ourselves imaginatively in another's place, sympathy. So, when we see someone harmed, we project ourselves into their situation and feel a desire to retaliate on their behalf. If this process is the source of our feelings about justice, that ought to undermine our confidence in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_distributive_justice">Theories of distributive justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Theories of distributive justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Distributive_justice" title="Distributive justice">Distributive justice</a></div>
<p>Theories of distributive justice need to answer three questions:
</p>
<ol><li><i>What goods</i> are to be distributed? Is it to be <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_power" title="Political power">power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respect" title="Respect">respect</a>, opportunities or some combination of these things?</li>
<li><i>Between what entities</i> are they to be distributed? Humans (dead, living, future), <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentient</a> beings, the members of a single society, <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a>?</li>
<li>What is the <i>proper</i> distribution? Equal, <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocratic</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">social status</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Need" title="Need">need</a>, based on property rights and non-aggression?</li></ol>
<p>Distributive justice theorists generally do not answer questions of <i>who has the right</i> to enforce a particular favored distribution, while property rights theorists say that there is no "favored distribution."  Rather, distribution should be based simply on whatever distribution results from lawful interactions or transactions (that is, transactions which are not illicit).
</p><p>This section describes some widely held theories of distributive justice, and their attempts to answer these questions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_justice">Social justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Social justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">Social justice</a></div>
<p>Social justice encompasses the just relationship between individuals and their society, often considering how privileges, opportunities, and wealth ought to be distributed among individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Social justice is also associated with <a href="/wiki/Social_mobility" title="Social mobility">social mobility</a>, especially the ease with which individuals and families may move between <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social strata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Social justice is distinct from <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitanism" title="Cosmopolitanism">cosmopolitanism</a>, which is the idea that all people belong to a single global community with a shared morality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Social justice is also distinct from <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarianism</a>, which is the idea that all people are equal in terms of status, value, or rights, as social justice theories do not all require equality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> For example, sociologist <a href="/wiki/George_C._Homans" title="George C. Homans">George C. Homans</a> suggested that the root of the concept of justice is that each person should receive rewards that are proportional to their contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Economist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> said that the concept of social justice was meaningless, saying that justice is a result of individual behavior and unpredictable market forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Social justice is closely related to the concept of relational justice, which is about the just relationship with individuals who possess features in common such as nationality, or who are engaged in cooperation or negotiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fairness">Fairness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fairness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Justice%22" rel="nofollow">"Justice"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Justice%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Justice%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Justice%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Justice%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Justice%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justice_statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="1728" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Justice_statue.jpg/220px-Justice_statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Justice_statue.jpg/330px-Justice_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Justice_statue.jpg/440px-Justice_statue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justice_statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>J. L. Urban, statue of <a href="/wiki/Lady_Justice" title="Lady Justice">Lady Justice</a> at court building in <a href="/wiki/Olomouc" title="Olomouc">Olomouc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a></div></div></div>
<p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Theory_of_Justice" title="A Theory of Justice">A Theory of Justice</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">John Rawls</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> argument to show that justice, and especially distributive justice, is a form of fairness: an impartial distribution of goods. Rawls asks us to imagine ourselves behind a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veil_of_ignorance" title="Veil of ignorance">veil of ignorance</a> that denies us all knowledge of our personalities, social statuses, moral characters, wealth, talents and life plans, and then asks what theory of justice we would choose to govern our society when the veil is lifted, if we wanted to do the best that we could for ourselves. We don't know who in particular we are, and therefore can't bias the decision in our own favour. So, the decision-in-ignorance models fairness, because it excludes selfish <a href="/wiki/Bias" title="Bias">bias</a>. Rawls said that each of us would reject the <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarian</a> theory of justice that we should maximize welfare (see below) because of the risk that we might turn out to be someone whose own good is sacrificed for greater benefits for others. Instead, we would endorse Rawls's <i>two principles of justice</i>:
</p>
<ul><li>Each person is to have an equal right to the most extensive total system of equal basic liberties compatible with a similar system of liberty for all.</li>
<li>Social and economic inequalities are to be arranged so that they are both
<ul><li>to the greatest benefit of the least advantaged, consistent with the just savings principle, and</li>
<li>attached to offices and positions open to all under conditions of fair equality of opportunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>This imagined choice justifies these principles as the principles of justice for us, because we would agree to them in a fair decision procedure. Rawls's theory distinguishes two kinds of goods – (1) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_(political)" title="Freedom (political)">the good of liberty rights</a> and (2) social and economic goods, i.e. wealth, income and power – and applies different distributions to them – equality between citizens for (1), equality unless inequality improves the position of the worst off for (2).
</p><p>In one sense, theories of distributive justice may assert that everyone should get what they deserve. Theories vary on the meaning of what is "deserved". The main distinction is between theories that say the basis of just deserts ought to be held equally by everyone, and therefore derive egalitarian accounts of distributive justice – and theories that say the basis of just deserts is unequally distributed on the basis of, for instance, hard work, and therefore derive accounts of distributive justice by which some should have more than others.
</p><p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocratic</a></i> theories, goods, especially wealth and <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">social status</a>, should be distributed to match individual <i>merit</i>, which is usually understood as some combination of talent and hard work. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Need" title="Need">needs</a></i>-based theories, goods, especially such basic goods as food, shelter and medical care, should be distributed to meet individuals' <a href="/wiki/Basic_needs" title="Basic needs">basic needs</a> for them. <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> is a needs-based theory, expressed succinctly in <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx's</a> slogan "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/From_each_according_to_his_ability,_to_each_according_to_his_need" title="From each according to his ability, to each according to his need">from each according to his ability, to each according to his need</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> According to <i>contribution</i>-based theories, goods should be distributed to match an individual's contribution to the overall social good.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Property_rights">Property rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Property rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">Libertarianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entitlement_theory" title="Entitlement theory">Entitlement theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_economics" title="Constitutional economics">Constitutional economics</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Anarchy,_State,_and_Utopia" title="Anarchy, State, and Utopia">Anarchy, State, and Utopia</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nozick" title="Robert Nozick">Robert Nozick</a> said that distributive justice is not a matter of the whole distribution matching an ideal <i>pattern</i>, but of each <a href="/wiki/Entitlement_theory" title="Entitlement theory">individual entitlement</a> having the right kind of <i>history</i>. It is just that a person has some good (especially, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Property_rights" title="Property rights">property right</a>) if and only if they came to have it by a history made up entirely of events of two kinds:
</p>
<ul><li>Just <i>acquisition</i>, especially by working on unowned things; and</li>
<li>Just <i>transfer</i>, that is free gift, sale or other agreement, but not <a href="/wiki/Theft" title="Theft">theft</a> (i.e. by force or fraud).</li></ul>
<p>If the chain of events leading up to the person having something meets this criterion, they are entitled to it: that they possess it is just, and what anyone else does or doesn't have or need is irrelevant.
</p><p>On the basis of this theory of distributive justice, Nozick said that all attempts to redistribute goods according to an ideal pattern, without the consent of their owners, are theft. In particular, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redistribution_(economics)" title="Redistribution (economics)">redistributive taxation</a> is theft.
</p><p>Some property rights theorists (such as Nozick) also take a consequentialist view of distributive justice and say that property rights based justice also has the effect of maximizing the overall wealth of an economic system.  They explain that voluntary (non-coerced) transactions always have a property called <a href="/wiki/Pareto_efficiency" title="Pareto efficiency">Pareto efficiency</a>. The result is that the world is better off in an absolute sense and no one is worse off.  They say that respecting property rights maximizes the number of Pareto efficient transactions in the world and minimized the number of non-Pareto efficient transactions in the world (i.e. transactions where someone is made worse off).  The result is that the world will have generated the greatest total benefit from the limited, scarce resources available in the world.  Further, this will have been accomplished without taking anything away from anyone unlawfully.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Welfare-maximization">Welfare-maximization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Welfare-maximization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">Utilitarianism</a></div>
<p>According to the utilitarian, justice requires the maximization of the total or average welfare across all relevant individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This may require sacrifice of some for the good of others, so long as everyone's good is taken impartially into account. Utilitarianism, in general, says that the standard of justification for actions, institutions, or the whole world, is <i>impartial welfare consequentialism</i>, and only indirectly, if at all, to do with <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a>, <a href="/wiki/Need" title="Need">need</a>, or any other non-utilitarian criterion. These other criteria might be indirectly important, to the extent that human welfare involves them. But even then, such demands as human rights would only be elements in the calculation of overall welfare, not uncrossable barriers to action.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_retributive_justice">Theories of retributive justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Theories of retributive justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Retributive_justice" title="Retributive justice">Retributive justice</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justicia_Ottawa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2948" data-file-width="1932" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Justicia_Ottawa.jpg/220px-Justicia_Ottawa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Justicia_Ottawa.jpg/330px-Justicia_Ottawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Justicia_Ottawa.jpg/440px-Justicia_Ottawa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justicia_Ottawa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Walter_Seymour_Allward" title="Walter Seymour Allward">Walter Seymour Allward</a>'s <i>Justitia</i> (Justice), outside <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Canada" title="Supreme Court of Canada">Supreme Court of Canada</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottawa,_Ontario" title="Ottawa, Ontario">Ottawa, Ontario</a> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a></div></div></div>
<p>Theories of retributive justice involve <a href="/wiki/Punishment" title="Punishment">punishment</a> for wrongdoing, and need to answer three questions:
</p>
<ol><li><i>why</i> punish?</li>
<li><i>who</i> should be punished?</li>
<li><i>what punishment</i> should they receive?</li></ol>
<p>This section considers the two major accounts of retributive justice, and their answers to these questions. <i>Utilitarian</i> theories look forward to the future consequences of punishment, while <i>retributive</i> theories look back to particular acts of wrongdoing, and attempt to balance them with deserved punishment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utilitarianism">Utilitarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Utilitarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the utilitarian, justice requires the maximization of the total or average welfare across all relevant individuals. Punishment fights crime in three ways:
</p>
<ol><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deterrence_(legal)" title="Deterrence (legal)">Deterrence</a></i>. The credible <a href="/wiki/Coercion" title="Coercion">threat</a> of punishment might lead people to make different choices; well-designed threats might lead people to make choices that maximize welfare. This matches some strong <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuitions</a> about just punishment: that it should generally be proportional to the crime.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rehabilitation_(penology)" title="Rehabilitation (penology)">Rehabilitation</a></i>. Punishment might make "bad people" into "better" ones. For the utilitarian, all that "bad person" can mean is "person who's likely to cause unwanted things (like suffering)". So, utilitarianism could recommend punishment that changes someone such that they are less likely to cause bad things.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Incapacitation_(penology)" title="Incapacitation (penology)">Security/Incapacitation</a></i>. Perhaps there are people who are irredeemable causers of bad things. If so, <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">imprisoning</a> them might maximize welfare by limiting their opportunities to cause harm and therefore the benefit lies within protecting society.</li></ol>
<p>So, the reason for punishment is the maximization of welfare, and punishment should be of whomever, and of whatever form and severity, are needed to meet that goal. This may sometimes justify punishing the innocent, or inflicting disproportionately severe punishments, when that will have the best consequences overall (perhaps executing a few suspected <a href="/wiki/Shoplifting" title="Shoplifting">shoplifters</a> live on television would be an effective deterrent to shoplifting, for instance). It also suggests that punishment might turn out <i>never</i> to be right, depending on the facts about what actual consequences it has.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retributivism">Retributivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Retributivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The retributivist will think <a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">consequentialism</a> is mistaken. If someone does something wrong we must respond by punishing for the committed action itself, regardless of what outcomes punishment produces. Wrongdoing must be balanced or made good in some way, and so the criminal <i>deserves</i> to be punished. It says that all guilty people, and only guilty people, deserve appropriate punishment. This matches some strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intuition_(knowledge)" title="Intuition (knowledge)">intuitions</a> about just punishment: that it should be <i>proportional</i> to the crime, and that it should be of <i>only</i> and <i>all of</i> the guilty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> However, it is sometimes said that retributivism is merely <a href="/wiki/Revenge" title="Revenge">revenge</a> in disguise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> However, there are differences between retribution and revenge: the former is impartial and has a scale of appropriateness, whereas the latter is personal and potentially unlimited in scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restorative_justice">Restorative justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Restorative justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Restorative_justice" title="Restorative justice">Restorative justice</a></div>
<p>Restorative justice (also sometimes called "reparative justice") is an approach to justice that focuses on the needs of victims and offenders, instead of satisfying abstract legal principles or punishing the offender. Victims take an active role in the process, while offenders are encouraged to take responsibility for their actions, "to repair the harm they've done – by apologizing, returning stolen money, or community service". It is based on a theory of justice that considers crime and wrongdoing to be an offense against an individual or community rather than the state. Restorative justice that fosters dialogue between victim and offender shows the highest rates of victim satisfaction and offender accountability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_theories">Mixed theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mixed theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some modern philosophers have said that Utilitarian and Retributive theories are not mutually exclusive. For example, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_von_Hirsch" title="Andrew von Hirsch">Andrew von Hirsch</a>, in his 1976 book <i>Doing Justice</i>, suggested that we have a moral obligation to punish greater crimes more than lesser ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> However, so long as we adhere to that constraint then utilitarian ideals would play a significant secondary role.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories">Theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bonino_da_Campione,_Justice,_c._1357,_NGA_46013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2221" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bonino_da_Campione%2C_Justice%2C_c._1357%2C_NGA_46013.jpg/220px-Bonino_da_Campione%2C_Justice%2C_c._1357%2C_NGA_46013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bonino_da_Campione%2C_Justice%2C_c._1357%2C_NGA_46013.jpg/330px-Bonino_da_Campione%2C_Justice%2C_c._1357%2C_NGA_46013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bonino_da_Campione%2C_Justice%2C_c._1357%2C_NGA_46013.jpg/440px-Bonino_da_Campione%2C_Justice%2C_c._1357%2C_NGA_46013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bonino_da_Campione,_Justice,_c._1357,_NGA_46013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonino da Campione, <i>Justice</i>, c. 1357, <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Justice_(virtue)" title="Justice (virtue)">Justice (virtue)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_virtues" title="Cardinal virtues">Cardinal virtues</a></div>
<p>It has been said<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> that 'systematic' or 'programmatic' political and moral philosophy in the West begins, in <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plato_Republic" title="Plato Republic">Republic</a>, with the question, 'What is Justice?'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> According to most contemporary theories of justice, justice is overwhelmingly important: <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">John Rawls</a> claims that "Justice is the first virtue of social institutions, as truth is of systems of thought."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In classical approaches, evident from <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> through to <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">Rawls</a>, the concept of 'justice' is always construed in logical or 'etymological' opposition to the concept of injustice. Such approaches cite various examples of injustice, as problems which a theory of justice must overcome.  A number of post-World War II approaches do, however, challenge that seemingly obvious dualism between those two concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Justice can be thought of as distinct from <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/benevolence" title="wikt:benevolence">benevolence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charity_(virtue)" title="Charity (virtue)">charity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prudence_(virtue)" title="Prudence (virtue)">prudence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercy" title="Mercy">mercy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Generosity" title="Generosity">generosity</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Compassion" title="Compassion">compassion</a>, although these dimensions are regularly understood to also be interlinked. Justice is the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_virtue" title="Cardinal virtue">cardinal virtues</a>, of which it is one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>  Metaphysical justice has often been associated with concepts of <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">fate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_Providence" title="Divine Providence">Divine Providence</a>, i.e., with a life in accordance with a cosmic plan.
</p><p>The equivalence of justice and fairness has been historically and culturally established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equality">Equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In political theory, liberalism includes two traditional elements: liberty and equality. Most contemporary theories of justice emphasize the concept of equality, including Rawls' theory of justice as fairness. For Ronald Dworkin, a complex notion of equality is the sovereign political virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Dworkin raises the question of whether society is under a duty of justice to help those responsible for the fact that they need help. Complications arise in distinguishing matters of choice and matters of chance, as well as justice for future generations in the redistribution of resources that he advocates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equality_before_the_law">Equality before the law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Equality before the law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Law raises important and complex issues about equality, fairness, and justice. There is an old saying that '<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=All_are_equal_before_the_law&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="All are equal before the law (page does not exist)">All are equal before the law</a>'. The belief in equality before the law is called legal egalitarianism. In criticism of this belief, the author <a href="/wiki/Anatole_France" title="Anatole France">Anatole France</a> said in 1894, "In its majestic equality, the law forbids <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rich_and_poor_alike" title="Rich and poor alike">rich and poor alike</a> to sleep under bridges, beg in the streets, and steal loaves of bread."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> With this saying, France illustrated the fundamental shortcoming of a theory of legal equality that remains blind to social inequality; the same law applied to all may have disproportionately harmful effects on the least powerful.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relational_justice">Relational justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Relational justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Relational justice seeks to examine the connections between individuals and focuses on their relations in societies, with respect to how these relationships are established and configured. In a normative view, this focus includes an understanding of what these relations should be. In a political view, this focus includes the method of organizing persons in society. Rawls’ theory of justice stakes out the task of justice as equalizing the distribution of primary social goods to benefit the worst-off in society. However, his distributive scheme, and other distributive accounts of justice do not directly consider power relations between and among individuals. Nor do they address such political considerations as various structures of decision-making, such as divisions of labor culture, or the construction of social meanings. Even Rawls’ own basic value of self-respect cannot be said to be amenable to distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Iris Marion Young charges that distributive accounts of justice fail to provide an adequate way of conceptualizing political justice in that they fail to take into account many of the demands of ordinary life and that a relational view of justice grounded upon understanding the differences among social groups offers a better approach, one which acknowledges unjust power relations among individuals, groups, and institutional structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Young Kim also takes a relational approach to the question of justice, but departs from Iris Marion Young's political advocacy of group rights and instead, he emphasizes the individual and moral aspects of justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> As to its moral aspects, he said that justice includes responsible actions based on rational and autonomous moral agency, with the individual as the proper bearer of rights and responsibilities. Politically, he maintains that the proper context for justice is a form of liberalism with the traditional elements of liberty and equality, together with the concepts of diversity and tolerance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_liberalism">Classical liberalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Classical liberalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Equality before the law is one of the basic principles of <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kukathas_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-kukathas-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-evans-36">[36]</a></sup> Classical liberalism calls for equality before the law, not for <a href="/wiki/Equality_of_outcome" title="Equality of outcome">equality of outcome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kukathas_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-kukathas-35">[35]</a></sup> Classical liberalism opposes pursuing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_rights" title="Group rights">group rights</a> at the expense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individual_rights" title="Individual rights">individual rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-evans-36">[36]</a></sup> In addition to equality, individual liberty serves as a core notion of classical liberalism. As to the liberty component, British social and political theorist, philosopher, and historian of ideas <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Berlin" title="Isaiah Berlin">Isaiah Berlin</a> identifies positive and negative liberty in "Two Concepts of Liberty",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> subscribing to a view of negative liberty, in the form of freedom from governmental interference. He further extends the concept of negative liberty in endorsing John Stuart Mills' harm principle: "the sole end for which mankind are warranted, individually and collectively, in interfering with the liberty of action of any of their number, is self-protection",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> which represents a classical liberal view of liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_spirituality">Religion and spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religion and spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abrahamic_justice">Abrahamic justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Abrahamic justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2659" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_079.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Moses with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tablets_of_Law" title="Tablets of Law">Tablets of Law</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt van Rijn</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslims</a> traditionally believe that justice is a present, real, right, and, specifically, governing concept along with <a href="/wiki/Mercy" title="Mercy">mercy</a>, and that justice is ultimately derived from and held by <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, such <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Moses" title="Law of Moses">Mosaic Law</a> were created by God to require the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> to live by and apply His standards of justice.
</p><p>The Hebrew Bible describes God as saying about the Judeo-Christian <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a> <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>: "No, for I have chosen him, that he may charge his children and his household after him to keep the way of the Lord by doing righteousness and justice;...." (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> 18:19, <a href="/wiki/New_Revised_Standard_Version" title="New Revised Standard Version">NRSV)</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalmist</a> describes God as having "Righteousness and justice [as] the foundation of [His] throne;...." (Psalms 89:14, NRSV).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> also describes God and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a> as having and displaying justice, often in comparison with God displaying and supporting <a href="/wiki/Mercy" title="Mercy">mercy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Matthew</a> 5:7).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_sentencing">Theories of sentencing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Theories of sentencing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(law)" title="Sentence (law)">sentence</a> forms the final explicit act of a <a href="/wiki/Judge" title="Judge">judge</a>-ruled process, and also the symbolic principal act connected to his function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The sentence can generally involve a decree of <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">imprisonment</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">fine</a> and/or other <a href="/wiki/Punishment" title="Punishment">punishments</a> against a <a href="/wiki/Defendant" title="Defendant">defendant</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conviction_(law)" title="Conviction (law)">convicted</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">crime</a>. Laws may specify the range of penalties that can be imposed for various offenses, and sentencing guidelines sometimes regulate what punishment within those ranges can be imposed given a certain set of offense and offender characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The most common purposes of sentencing in legal theory are:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr style="background:#b0c4de; text-align:center;">
<th>Theory
</th>
<th>Aim of theory
</th>
<th>Suitable punishment
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Retributive_justice" title="Retributive justice">Retribution</a>
</td>
<td>Punishment imposed for no reason other than an offense being committed, on the basis that if <a href="/wiki/Eye_for_an_eye" title="Eye for an eye">proportionate</a>, punishment is morally acceptable as a response that satisfies the aggrieved party, their intimates and society.
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Tariff sentences</li>
<li>Sentence must be proportionate to the crime</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deterrence_(legal)" title="Deterrence (legal)">Deterrence</a>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>To the individual – the individual is deterred through fear of further punishment.</li>
<li>To the general public – Potential offenders warned as to likely punishment</li></ul>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Prison Sentence</li>
<li>Heavy Fine</li>
<li>Long sentence as an example to others</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Rehabilitation_(penology)" title="Rehabilitation (penology)">Rehabilitation</a>
</td>
<td>To reform the offender's behavior
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Individualized sentences</li>
<li>Community service orders</li>
<li>moral education</li>
<li>vocational education</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Incapacitation_(penology)" title="Incapacitation (penology)">Incapacitation</a>
</td>
<td>Offender is made incapable of committing further crime to protect society at large from crime
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Long prison sentence</li>
<li>Electronic tagging</li>
<li>Banning orders</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Reparation_(legal)" title="Reparation (legal)">Reparation</a>
</td>
<td>Repayment to victim(s) or to community
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Compensation</li>
<li>Unpaid work</li>
<li>Reparation Schemes</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Denunciation_(penology)" title="Denunciation (penology)">Denunciation</a>
</td>
<td>Society expressing its disapproval reinforcing moral boundaries
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Reflects blameworthiness of offense</li>
<li>punishment in public</li>
<li>punishment reported to public</li></ul>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(common_law)" title="Civil law (common law)">civil cases</a> the decision is usually known as a <a href="/wiki/Verdict" title="Verdict">verdict</a>, or judgment, rather than a sentence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Civil cases are settled primarily by means of monetary compensation for harm done ("<a href="/wiki/Damages" title="Damages">damages</a>") and orders intended to prevent future harm (for example <a href="/wiki/Injunction" title="Injunction">injunctions</a>). Under some legal systems an award of damages involves some scope for retribution, denunciation and deterrence, by means of additional categories of damages beyond simple compensation, covering a <a href="/wiki/Punitive_damages" title="Punitive damages">punitive effect, social disapprobation, and potentially, deterrence</a>, and occasionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disgorgement_(law)" title="Disgorgement (law)">disgorgement</a> (forfeit of any gain, even if no loss was caused to the other party).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_perspectives">Evolutionary perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Evolutionary perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="544" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg/220px-Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg/330px-Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg/440px-Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gerechtigkeit-1537.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Justice as a naked woman with a <a href="/wiki/Sword" title="Sword">sword</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">balance</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a>, 1537</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_ethic" title="Evolutionary ethic">Evolutionary ethics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_morality" title="Evolution of morality">evolution of morality</a> suggest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary" title="Evolutionary">evolutionary</a> bases for the concept of justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biosocial_criminology" title="Biosocial criminology">Biosocial criminology</a> research says that human perceptions of what is appropriate criminal justice are based on how to respond to crimes in the ancestral small-group environment and that these responses may not always be appropriate for today's societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions_to_fairness">Reactions to fairness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Reactions to fairness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justitia,_Jost_Amman.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="830" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Justitia%2C_Jost_Amman.png/220px-Justitia%2C_Jost_Amman.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Justitia%2C_Jost_Amman.png/330px-Justitia%2C_Jost_Amman.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Justitia%2C_Jost_Amman.png/440px-Justitia%2C_Jost_Amman.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justitia,_Jost_Amman.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Justitia", copper engraving by <a href="/wiki/Jost_Amman" title="Jost Amman">Jost Amman</a>, made between 1539 and 1591</div></div></div>
<p>Studies at <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles" title="University of California, Los Angeles">UCLA</a> in 2008 have indicated that reactions to fairness are "wired" into the brain and that, "Fairness is activating the same part of the brain that responds to food in rats... This is consistent with the notion that being treated fairly satisfies a basic need".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Research conducted in 2003 at <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory University</a> involving capuchin monkeys demonstrated that other cooperative animals also possess such a sense and that "<a href="/wiki/Inequity_aversion" title="Inequity aversion">inequity aversion</a> may <a href="/wiki/Inequity_aversion_in_animals" title="Inequity aversion in animals">not be</a> uniquely human".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_and_justice">Institutions and justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Justice&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Institutions and justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">Law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3692" data-file-width="2805" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg/220px-Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg/330px-Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg/440px-Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_pope_Paulus_V_Vatican_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coat_of_Arms" title="Coat of Arms">Coat of Arms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_V" title="Pope Paul V">Pope Paul V</a>, ceiling of the room of the geographical maps, <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4272" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG/220px-27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG/330px-27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG/440px-27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:27._%C3%89glise_Saint-Paul_Montlu%C3%A7on.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stained glass of the Saint-Paul church in <a href="/wiki/Montlu%C3%A7on" title="Montluçon">Montluçon</a> <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1770" data-file-width="1716" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg/220px-Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg/330px-Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg/440px-Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allegoria_della_Giustizia_-_galleria_del_Poccetti.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Allegory of Justice. Ceiling of galleria del Poccetti in the Palazzo Pitti (Florence)</div></div></div>
<p>In a world where people are interconnected but they disagree, institutions are required to instantiate ideals of justice. These institutions may be justified by their approximate instantiation of justice, or they may be deeply unjust when compared with ideal standards – consider the institution of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>. Justice is an ideal the world fails to live up to, sometimes due to deliberate opposition to justice despite understanding, which could be disastrous. The question of institutive justice raises issues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political_science)" title="Legitimacy (political science)">legitimacy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_procedure" title="Legal procedure">procedure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statutory_interpretation" title="Statutory interpretation">interpretation</a>, which are considered by legal theorists and by <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_law" title="Philosophy of law">philosophers of law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The United Nations <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_16" title="Sustainable Development Goal 16">Sustainable Development Goal 16</a> emphasizes the need for strong institutions in order to uphold justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>

<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20091204190016/http://unrol.org/article.aspx?article_id=30" rel="nofollow">United Nations Rule of Law: Informal Justice</a>, on the relationship between informal/community justice, the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.justiceharvard.org" rel="nofollow">Justice: What's The Right Thing To Do?</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140427043908/http://www.justiceharvard.org/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 27 April 2014 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, a series of 12 videos on the subject of justice by Harvard University's Michael Sandel, with reading materials and comments from participants.</li></ul>
